Craig's Bond.  In the beginning he was promoted as more violent, less gentlemanly, more unrestrained in Masculinity, less interested in the trappings of fashion and even ladies.  He was a serious agent, a military man of extremely brutal action.  This new reality was proven quickly - the visceral, brutal fight in the toilet in Casino Royale.  His opponent was a young, physically imposing man, agile and able.  Bond had to unleash full MASCULINE power to subdue him.  It's there.  You can't fluff it when you want to emphasis that this Bond is grittier and more real.  The brutality in that toilet was Masculine.  No female would deny it. So, if the female 007, however athletic she appeared to be, walked into that toilet to face that trapped man, who would walk out?  I know who would walk out, reality knows who would walk out.  So the Reality that began Craig's time as Bond actually regressed by the final film, and we were back to a fantasy world where women easily mix it in physical terms with trained athletic men.  The inference of her being a 00 agent is that she could have been as physically dominant in that toilet as Bond was. So where's the progress?  Fantasy is not progress.  Fantasy is a lie.  As much a lie as the ladies of the 60s, there only as window dressing on an unmade bed.   How then would I get an action woman in there more realistically in this last movie?  Well, Bond was retired.  He and Moneypenny have a platonic relationship/friendship that was never developed in these movies.  And why did Naomi Harris, a black woman, still have to be door opener in Craigs's last Bond?  Again - progress? They should have spent time together as friends, during which time Bond would have taught her some of his techniques etc.  She would not have become a 007, but the story might have led to her needing some of the stuff he'd taught her.  A strong black female role, Naomi Harris being taken off door opening duty, Naomi Harris being a bigger part of a Bond tale - she deserved it -- and bluntly, her being the female and black action figure.  Everyone happy.  Even me!  Naomi Harris can do no wrong in my book.

FINALLY saw this tonight. When it hit theaters I was too busy. Caught a VERY subtle nod to Casino Royale. When Rami Malek first visits Madeline in her office, he points out a Foxglove in her office. Kind of pinkish, purplish flower, very common in Europe, and invasive in parts of the US. Foxglove is of the genus Digitalis, which is the poison that Le Chiffre tried to kill Bond with at Casino Royale. Neither film ties Digitalis and Foxglove together. Tough one to catch, but for Botany/Flower nerds.   Really good film. Better than I expected. Lea Seydoux was excellent. I think Ralph Fiennes did his best 'M' work so far. *     Hi Chris. Say, I think I may have figured out how you can work the rest of the James Bond films in with the Daniel Craig films *and* make it work. First of all, the James Bond video game called "007 Legends" provides us with the answer of how to work it all out. When you watch the non-Daniel Craig Bond films, you have to assume that it's the same man. What it does, is it gives you an idea of what the Daniel Craig Bond might've been like, in those different eras.  *     To get a better idea of what I'm talking about, play or do research on the video game "007 Legends" and that'll give you a much better idea. The writers of the video game only made one mistake. All of the other Bond films would've had to have taken place in between the films "Skyfall", and "Spectre". *Not* in between "Quantum of Solace" and "Skyfall". So, in other words, the entire James Bond(film) chronology, would play out something like this: "Casino Royale", "Quantum of Solace", "Skyfall".  *     This would be followed by "The Living Daylights"(because the Timothy Dalton Bond films are considered to be prequels of the Sean Connery Bond films), "Licence to Kill"(where they mention another wife that Bond had. Could they be referring to Vesper?), "Goldeneye"(because the Pierce Brosnan Bond films are considered to be a continuation of the Timothy Dalton Bond films), "Tomorrow Never Dies", "The World is Not Enough", "Die Another Day", "Dr. No", "From Russia with Love"(where Bond hears about SPECTRE for the first time).  *     Then after that, would be "Goldfinger"(which takes place *after* Moneypenny becomes "M's" secretary), "Thunderball", "You Only Live Twice"(where Bond meets Blofeld for the first time), "Diamonds are Forever"(where Bond encounters Blofeld 3 times), "Never Say Never Again"(where Bond goes up against Emilio Largo's son "Maximilian Largo"), "On Her Majesty's Secret Service"(where he meets Blofeld, presumably while in disguise, much like he was disguised in "You Only Live Twice), "Live and Let Die", "The Man with the Golden Gun".  *     After that, it would be "The Spy Who Loved Me", "Moonraker", "For Your Eyes Only"(where Bond drops Blofeld into a smokestack, but perhaps Blofeld's wheelchair had some gadgets on it that may have saved his life and gotten him outta there), "Octopussy", "A View to a Kill"(which would be his very last mission right before the Bond film called "Spectre").  *     Finally, there would be "Spectre"(where he finally sees Blofeld again after last seeing him in "For Your Eyes Only". Why doesn't Bond comment on this, or any of their other previous encounters? Probably because even by the "Dr. No" mission, Bond was already starting to sound like he was getting burned out with these dictators who think they're Napoleon, or........God. Then he goes on to say in a very bemused tone of voice ".....same old dream........world domination."  *     So in other words, Bond would not want to open the door for Blofeld to engage him in idle chit chat. By the time of "Spectre", he had enough of the guy. He didn't wanna chat him up. He wanted to kill him. He was obviously too dangerous to be kept alive. What about the scars? He obviously had the scar tissue removed, only to be replaced by new, very similar scars.  *     Why didn't Blofeld comment on their previous encounters?¬†He probably had no interest in walking down memory lane with Bond. He hated Bond. Bond had humiliated him several times, but Blofeld certainly didn't want him to have a quick, painless death. Not by any stretch of the imagination), and then finally, there is "No Time to Die". So, there you have it. That is literally the only way that you can work out the James Bond chronology at this point. Now........it's time for another reboot. Heaven help us............

First of all can I say I really like your approach to reviewing a movie.  No hysteria nor aggressive anger.    Now as to the film my thoughts, having only seen it two hours ago and then just now this review, is that I don't think I would know where to rate it among Daniel Craig's other Bond outings.  The reason being is that is felt so different and quite unique.  Rami Malek's character I enjoyed because his evil came from a different place and he had an almost pathetic tragic presence which is a nice change.  Lea Seydoux was simply beautiful, not just in appearance but in every gaze, gesture and vibe - you really felt her pain and sorrow.  Also the chemistry between her character and Daniel Craig's seemed so real, passionate and full of complexity and depth.  The chemistry between the two leads had a lot more strength to it than the Bond/Vesper pairing ).  This was a very emotional movie, similar to Skyfall in that respect, however I thought this offered even more.  No Time to Die had enormous emotional pull yet more importantly had moments of beauty.  For me this perhaps makes it the best James Bond movie ever!  Hans Zimmer's score was perfect and the reprise of We Have All the Time in the World from the early Bond film, On Her Majesty's Secret Service was a brilliant idea, especially seeing the last five minutes of that other film was also emotionally powerful.  I think it will depend on what the viewer wants most out of a Bond film that will decide his or her favourite.  As a thriller I would go with Casino Royale, for back story Skyfall but for romance, tragedy (sense of loss) and for the love of the title character then No Time to Die is a masterpiece.  P.S. Yes A View to a Kill is best taken as a comedy.  Also yes, Billie Irish's song is profound and perfect for this film.

I dunno, I went in hoping for another solid Craig Bond film, but I left the theater feeling pretty unfulfilled.

I thought it was one of the best if not the best bond film at least in my mind. I think producers definitely made some bold decisions to really tackle the "traditional" bond formula that they used over and over in this franchise. This may be the controversial talking point but I liked the fact that they really wanted to cast a new light on bond's traditional character as a playboy for whom women are just an accessory and a means to satisfy sexual desires into more of a family man and a father who genuinely falls in love with one woman and wanting to protect the family, which is something we as an audience are not quite familiar with. The way they ended the movie was also bold and signifies not only the end of Daniel Craig's era as a bond but also leaves the audience curious as to where the franchise will go next and opens up a lot of exciting possibilities. I think bond franchise has definitely come to realise that it is time to break the shell and try new things accordingly with the changing world. I applaud their courage to do so because it can be viewed as a risky move. The storyline was believable and I was immensely impressed to watch Daniel Craig's portrayal of bond mature through each movie. Cinematography was also beautiful. Overall I would give 9/10.

It has flaws: the way the Hong Kong assassins were portrayed is rather poor and the way they are called "Orientals" doesn't hold up, and the way in the end Felix is all happy and jolly and OK is too jarring after what happened to him and the big intense climax.  HOWEVER, that's where it ends. For the rest, I found "No Time To Die" to have way more issues and WTF moments than "License to Kill" (spoilers ahead):  -The whole thing about Blofeld setting up the trap at Vesper's grave. How the fuck does that work? It's such an asspull when you stop and really think about it. For a series that takes itself more seriously, it's sure pretty laughably unrealistic. -The movie is too long, it's not awful, but it really overstays its welcome and should have trimmed down in places. -None of the female heroines hold a candle to Pam Bouvier, who is still to this day the best Bond girl ever. AGAIN???) but even on a writing sense they're cluttered (OK, I get you want to destroy SPECTRE for what they did to you, but why kill millions? He never explains himself), and they don't hold a candle to LTK's villains who sure, are drug dealers, but do it in a unique and creative way, and how Sanchez is an extremely charismatic leader but also a scary boss who takes faithfulness very seriously and Bond smartly uses this against his allies. They have a more lean and well written evil scheme that is nicely wrapped up by the end without leaving headscratchers of feeling undercooked. And even as performances they don't hold a candle to Sanchez and his creepy but awesome henchman Dario. And the way they get taken care of, especially Blofeld and Safin, is such a soft fart of a sendoff. Far cry from Dario getting fuckin' thrown in a grinder turning the cocaine dust red, or Sanchez being burned alive and blown up. Why are films these days SO afraid of being creative and memorable?  Yes, it's a better final Bond film that what Moore, Connery and Brosnan did. But much like how people forget that Timothy Dalton was the original darker and edgier Bond, they also seem to forget that he also had the original GOOD final film. I think the main problem people have is with the casual passing on of the 007 monicker. If you read Fleming‚Äôs original books, 00 numbers can‚Äôt just be inherited or passed around like baseball cards. They‚Äôre a rare set of agents who distinguish themselves in an assignment by killing someone in cold blood to complete it. They basically have to show that they‚Äôre willing to do the dirty work of the government in order to be selected as international men of mystery. You can‚Äôt just inherit a 00 number.

This seems to be going around in the comments section but I didn‚Äôt expect to be so emotionally moved by a James Bond film. There are two reasons for this, like a lot of people here, I grew up with James Bond around me. In fact, when I asked my mom if she still liked James Bond, she said ‚Äúoh yeah! I‚Äôve seen them all! Even the old ones‚Äú and it was one of those franchises that we could enjoy together even during periods of my wife when we didn‚Äôt really get along. It was one of the few things that sort of made things OK for a little while. And that made it possible for things to be OK between us on a more permanent basis. But aside from that, it was also one of the things that my grandfather enjoyed before he passed away.  He was someone who was unfortunate enough to be a child when the Japanese occupied the Philippines during World War II, he then enlisted in the US military and served in both Vietnam and Korea. And it was these reasons that James Bond hit me so hard, when it came to Daniel Craig‚Äòs concluding film. Daniel Craig is the Christian Bale of the Bond Franchise. His Bond films are The Dark Knight Trilogy of the franchise. Craig made Bond, dark, gritty and a believable character again. He brought the franchise back to it's roots. Just like Christian Bale and Christopher Nolan did with the Batman character. Casino Royale is the Bond version of Batman Begins. It gave fresh new life to the Bond Franchise, and brought it back from the goofiness and the over the top galore of Die Another Day. Just like Batman Begins did it after the backlash with Batman & Robin. Craig made the Bond films, dark, believable and realistic again. Also you could say that No Time to Die is the Bond version of The Dark Knight Rises. As Bond sacrifices himself to save the world just like Batman did it in The Dark Knight Rises. Both the Bond and Batman films have had a similar evolution in tone and depiction. By initially starting off as dark and gritty, then becoming camp and over the top. Then becoming dark, grounded, realistic and serious in tone again.

Last year, I created a Bond project for my 9th grade students here in Norway. They read about Ian Fleming and the creation of the character, I taught them about music, cinematography and previous Bond films and actors. Then we watched the first four Daniel Craig movies, wrote reviews and watched your reviews here on YouTube. Finally, we had a conversation about their own experiences with the project and the films. Three weeks ago, we all went to the theatre and watched "No Time to Die" together, as a conclusion to the Daniel Craig era and our Bond experience. It was well received by my students (now 10th graders) and I am so happy that we went together and shared the experience. Thanks to you for many good reviews, Chris. Keep 'em coming!

I WANTED to like the ending.  I WANTED to feel like it was an oddly appropriate sendoff to a very different iteration of Bond.  But I can't.  I HATE it.  Without any particular big expectations or anxious fixation on the film (which tells me I didn't just build up unreasonable expectations or buy into any hype), I quietly designated No Time To Die as my personal celebratory first trip back to the theater to mark and enjoy SOMETHING like a return to "post pandemic normalcy" with some escapist fun from my fave film franchise.  Instead I got a full-force kick in the balls from a self-serious melodrama so hellbent on playing with the boundaries of what a "Bond movie" even IS that it became something else entirely.  And rather than having enjoyed a fun diversion and come back feeling invigorated from a thrill ride, I've returned home feeling saddened and DEPRESSED.  It certainly isn't that Craig's interpretation of Bond just "wasn't for me."  I actually liked his grittier take on the character, complete with a little bit of humanizing vulnerability and cynicism about his job.  When others were complaining early on that Craig's Bond was a sad-sack lacking "fun" I was still fully enjoying the movies and appreciating how much more relatable they'd managed to make the character.  Rather than bow to the modern puritans who demand all flawed characters be "rehabilitated" or have "problematic" behaviors stripped away, the writers not only dared to keep Bond's  , but to use the Craig reboot to take Bond's vices and womanizing an subtly show them as coping mechanisms for a man with a psychologically damaging job (whereas the older films shy away from Bond's inner world to the point that his drinking and sleeping around come off simply as wantonly edgy, "bad boy" behavior for the sake of making him seem cool and sexy.)  But around that different take on *the character* the Craig films BEGAN as something which was still true to its popcorn entertainment roots with some elements of thrills and fun.  But from around the middle of Skyfall, they began a slog towards trying to transform *the tone of the series itself* into more drama than action or fun.  And I've gone steadily from thinking Craig was the BEST Bond to lamenting the complete transformation of the series far beyond the daring, but limited reinterpretation Craig began.  They started out going refreshingly off the beaten path just a bit, but kept going until they ran off the edge of the map.  No Time To Die feels like an art house melodrama with action elements which had the Bond logo and a cheap publicity stunt ending slapped on it.  I resent them for so humanizing Craig's Bond for so long, giving him a wife and child in the eleventh hour just to gut everyone with a contrived gimmick of  an ending.  I'd have reluctantly bought him DECEIVING his wife and daughter that he wasn't going to make it to spare them the pain of having to live apart forever due to the taint of the bioweapon, which would have still achieved bittersweet tragedy while showing Bond's growth and nobility, as well as his tragic resignation that no one he loves will ever be safe near him.  I'd have expected this ending from the series finale of a show like 24--in fact I wrongly predicted they'd have the stones to do it.  But when you publicly aim to continue the franchise?  NTTD's ending is unexpected not because it is unpredictable and daring, but because it is a cynical subversive trick.  It is a suckerpunch, not daring storytelling.  If it weren't for having gotten to see Ana De Armas looking as graceful and stunning as any woman should ever be CAPABLE of, I might have been tempted to ask for a refund.

I'm 29 years old, I grew up on Bond like every other Bond fan. Seeing Casino Royale in theaters in 2006 when I was in 7th grade is the definitive cinema experience of my youth, I will always cherish that moment, much like how people feel when they saw Star Wars in theaters for the first time as a kid. The franchise as a whole means so much to me, and Daniel Craigs tenure will always be my favorite. No Time to Die is exactly what I wanted from his finale. Every action scene and car chase is phenomonal. Its hilarious. It's bittersweet. It's heart wrenching. And the fan service is so well done. There are so many nods to the franchise, but they never feel out of place, and they are so subtle that I'm sure an casual Bond fan wouldn't even notice. But what I love most is that it is a soft remake of a certain Bond film from the past, (I won't say for spoilers). Any hardcore Bond fan knows that film in particular is a giant outlier in the series. I couldn't think of a better way to end Daniel's tenure because I think his films will always also be considered a giant outlier in the franchise. Ending his run this way just feels so fitting and it really feels like we've come full circle on the franchise as a whole. I'm sad that it's over, but I'm beyond happy that it happened, and I'm so excited for the future of the franchise. Thank you Barbara. Thank you Cary. And most of all, thank you Daniel.
Rami Malek character had me bored to death. Other than that I thought it was like you said, the best close to a bond series.. I consider this and Casino Royale good bookends to an otherwise extremely forgettable franchise.
Billie Eilisch is talented, but I wish she would enunciate more clearly when she sings. She‚Äôs very numbly and mush-mouthed, Like Sam Smith (yikes!)

You and I had vastly different experiences as Bond fans it seems, which is perfectly fine, just a bit strange. I thought as a movie it was incoherent and messy. Characters were once again written shallow and single-layered. The action was very stagey, which is a thing in Bond movies, but the action genre has produced far better and more natural flows for the past 20 years. The foggy forest scene do stand out though, just too short with a poor reward at the end. The black lady agent, though better than expected, still made me think she was shoe-horned politically rather than practically, which is perhaps more a flaw on my side given the oversensitive times we live in than the actual movie, but her character was so weak and redundant, so perhaps not.  I think it is one of the worst Bond movies because of how little it thinks of the intelligence of its audience. It is very childish at times, inconceivably immature in its reasoning of sequences, technology, the dialogue. While I was watching the very serious narrative about a biotech weapon by a terrorist organization hell-bent on destroying the modern institutions that safeguard democracies seemed out of touch with the reality of the current world, where nationalist accomplish the same by just spreading propaganda through social media platforms at a fraction of the cost. I simply think Bond has unfortunately run its course as a serious franchise, because the real-world backdrop does not allow it the same level of detachment post-2015, which makes it difficult to take the premise of the movie seriously when you know that once the credits roll, the actual threat resumes. Yes, movies are about escapism and Bond movies exist on some parallel universe setting, but it has always been a running theme for Bond films to draw inspiration from real political world affairs, perfectly exemplified in shitty, but relevant Tomorrow Never Dies where the villain is personifying fake media/propaganda. Again, I think you sometimes have these blind spots for franchises you care about and it prevents you from judging them appropriately harsh when need be.  To me, this was just another demonstration of a very high budget action movie with brains-off plot, script, characters and flow. I expect more from Bond movies, I don't know why, but with the exception of Casino Royale, they have not delivered under Craig.

For me, Spectre was a terrible film that would have been better served without the comic shenanigans (like Bond falling goofily onto a couch in the opening action set piece), Blofeld being unnecessarily related to Bond and every one of his adventures, and the over-the-top tropes (like the skull drill that Blofeld tortured Bond with). If the studio (or the creatives) wanted a more retro feeling film, they should have waited until the end of Craig's tenure to do one. As it stands (to me) Spectre is a willfully silly movie stuck in the middle of a self-serious and grounded Bond story. And it throws everything off. Watching all of the films back-to-back, it REALLY stands out as the one that feels as if it shouldn't exist in the narrative. Especially now that No Time to Die, which seems to very much share the same tone as Casino, Quantum and Skyfall is ending the narrative.  For me, if you have a series of movies, books, games, etc. that are supposed to be telling a single story; sharing a singular narrative thread, consistency between entries is paramount. (The MCU being an exception of course because "comic books". And even then they still manage to FEEL as if they're all a part of the same narrative due to visual cues and the quality of the writing.)  My personal order would be:  Skyfall, Casino, Quantum and Spectre. (I won't rank No Time until I see it, but I have a feeling it will either be before or after Casino given word of mouth.)  Just my personal opinion.

Chris, I'm sorry to say, but you are _wrong_ about the 00 number: It is _not_ 'just a number'. According to Ian Fleming's books, and confirmed in the movies, every 00 number is inextricably connected to the individual carrying it, even if they retire or die. Every new 00 agent gets the _next_ consecutive number. Same goes for the name - James Bond is not a pseudonym, it is the _real_ name of the character. So now that he's supposedly dead, I'm not sure how they want to go about the next movie.  Another thing I have to say here, the problem with the alleged 'agenda catering' was clearly incited by the way the early trailers were made, in which it seemed like James Bond's character would get demolished, and his role, name, and number taken over by a black female, and it's quite understandable why people got upset with this - not because a black female super spy wouldn't be an interesting idea if the character is well written, but because James Bond 007 has been established over decades (and by the source material) as a white male.  An argument could also be made about female and male roles in this movie: Every relevant male character in this movie was either a villain (Safin, Ash, Primo, Blofeld, and the scientist), irresponsibly negligent (M), gay (Q), or got killed (Bond and Leiter), whereas all the female characters either were great at what they did (Nomi, Paloma, and Moneypenny), or got forced by men to do immoral things (Madeleine).  Personally, I'm of the opinion that this could very well be coincidence, and I really don't care that much about it anyway, as long as the story is decent. I enjoyed the movie quite a bit, the action and performance of _all_ actors was near perfect, the cinematography on a very high level, and the soundtrack was great. The only thing I'd criticize would be that I wish Safin's background and motivation would have been fleshed out more, and the nano-virus will create a big issue if (hopefully) the next JB is the same person.  Overall, I'd give this movie a 7/10, maybe even 8/10. Not the best Bond movie, but clearly one of the better ones.  PS: I agree on your statement on Ana de Armas. I hope they will do more with her.

I'm sorry Chris, but that didn't feel like a review. I felt like you enjoyed a big theatrical release. That does not make it a good film. I saw the film in a theater, a week after seeing Dune. Here are my thoughts (with SPOILERS):   Unfortunately, it's the weakest Bond film, especially when it comes to it's writing. No doubt the sound, action and locations are breathtaking, but those are limited. There are endless plot holes and issues in this film. Firstly, the character of Bond being a lonely, depressed lover start bothering you real quick. He is literally sad for 2 hours and 45 minutes. Rami Malek was wasted in this film, he was barely present. His motivations are extremely unconvincing and the film doesn't bother giving any clarity whatsoever about his backstory - it seems like they weren't interested in that element of the film or it was unimportant, which is strange. He is a phenomenal actor and could have been a great villain, but he was neither given scope nor explored. Bond's best sequences in the film are with Ana de Armas, sadly they share the screen for about 5 - 10 minutes only. The length and pacing of the film were really testing the audience's patience. The film's threat discusses a virus that would attack certain DNA and destroy races/people/humanity. It's 2021 and they expect the audience to buy into something so ridiculous? Quite childish. The scientist who was used as 'comic relief' (and to give out a random racist remark) was unnecessary and in poor taste. By the point they reveal Bond has a daughter, it was turning to a sappy Bollywood movie. Bond's sacrificing act didn't work for me, because I wasn't invested in the film anymore. I was so shocked at the ending and extremely angry. Was Rami Malek really that villain who could kill Bond? And we know what about him? Almost nothing. How is that a good film? At the end of the day, who wants to watch a Bond film in which 90% of the time Agent 007 is not James Bond, but somebody else? Call me silly, but for a Bond fan like me, 0-0-7 aren't just numbers. I wanted to have a hell of time going into this movie, but I'm better off watching Skyfall or Casino Royale again. This is not the Bond the audiences deserve during the pandemic. Disappointing.

I haven't seen the film, so I don't know if this happens, but you can't just replace a character with this kind of trajectory and expect people to not get upset.  Remember Metal Gear Solid 2. An excellent game, but lambasted because they replaced traditional protagonist Solid Snake for newbie Raiden. Raiden wasn't a bad character, but he wasn't Snake, so people got considerably upset. This was a strange one for me... on the one hand, I put on my objective film critic hat, and I can see that this is a well-made movie that in some ways might be one of the best, if not THE best James Bond movie for how well-crafted and emotionally impactful it is...  ...but then, I put on my Bond fan hat, or even just my ‚Äúaction/adventure movie fan‚Äù hat, and I felt like I was left rather cold from this movie. It doesn‚Äôt really feel like a Bond movie to me. I feel like the weight of all the traditions that the Craig era has either done away with or subverted or ‚Äúevolved‚Äù over the course of his run, have taken their toll on how this movie feels in the franchise. It was already a problem for me in Spectre, where the movie took on a very slow, dramatic, ‚Äúmature‚Äù and strangely colorless feel to it, which is strange for me when it comes to Bond. Skyfall had managed to hit a more mature and ‚Äúclassy‚Äù level of filmmaking, while still retaining enough action, edge and vigor to make it still feel like a Bond movie. Spectre took that approach a step further, but lost the vigor, IMO. The movie was boring. No Time To Die isn‚Äôt quite boring, but I just found myself feeling surprisingly blah from it, even during most of the action. And again, I put on my objective reviewer hat, it‚Äôs all very well-done, but something about an aging Craig running around in a dark depressing forest trying to save his ‚Äúwife and kid‚Äù just doesn‚Äôt feel Bond to me. It felt like a generic family thriller instead. A well-made one, but a generic one, nonetheless.  The long, slow, more dramatic pre-title sequence didn‚Äôt really help either. I really felt the lack of momentum that I usually want from a Bond opening sequence. I think this is the longest and slowest pre-title sequence ever, which takes well over 10 minutes or so? to get to the actual action sequence. And then the title song by Billie Eilish... I mean, I heard it a year or so ago, when it first came out, like everybody did... so I knew what to expect on that going in, and I was just as bored by it as I expected. I‚Äôm tired of the slow, dramatic Bond songs. I‚Äôm really craving another ‚ÄúYou Know My Name‚Äù type rock song that will actually kick off a film properly, the way that song did for Casino Royale.   Speaking of which, I actually came from No Time To Die with a huge craving to watch Casino Royale, just so I could get a taste of that more upbeat, fun, vigorous, bright, colorful and exciting version of Bond that I really am missing these days. I get that Craig‚Äôs Bond evolved from that, and in some ways, he‚Äôs a better character on a dramatic level. But I‚Äôm just not in the mood for that these days, for whatever reason. I didn‚Äôt really expect this movie would be the kind of Bond movie I want, but I at least expected to be more engaged by it, and I just wasn‚Äôt really, even though I can objectively see that the movie and it‚Äôs emotional content is well-done, as well as the action being well done... but I just couldn‚Äôt get into it very much for the same reason I couldn‚Äôt get into Spectre... too drab in it‚Äôs filmmaking style. Too colorless and lifeless a color palette... everything‚Äôs white, black, brown or gray. This one took on more dark lighting too. Too slow and long in its pacing. Not enough hallmarks of Bond, and when they‚Äôre there, they‚Äôre too watered down and ‚Äúmature‚Äù in their approach, even while tongue-in-cheek repeatedly paying homage to the fact that they‚Äôre NOT doing it the traditional way, which is an approach that was novel and interesting in Casino Royale, even up until Skyfall, but by this point, I get it... he‚Äôs not our father‚Äôs Bond... but I kinda want him to be a bit more???  I dunno... I hope I come around and can appreciate this movie for what it is, but I found myself more depressed that Casino Royale, and to a lesser extent Skyfall, are the only films of Craig‚Äôs objectively strong run as Bond that I can actually feel the ‚ÄúBond‚Äù vibe from, and I found myself really nostalgically longing for at least one more Casino Royale style film from him, or perhaps an even more typical Bond film that would be more reminiscent of the Connery era, or even a good Brosnan film like GoldenEye... and I didn‚Äôt get it. Oh well... I guess it just makes me appreciate Casino Royale all the more.  I don‚Äôt know who would be best for the next Bond, but I just hope that whoever it is... they can start off with a good, exciting, fresh movie again, like GoldenEye or Casino Royale. I really want that exciting classic Bond feel from a fresh, new, modern movie again.
Apart from Casino Royale, I haven't enjoyed the Craig era at all, and I'm glad it's finally coming to a close.   Hopefully, we can get some fun injected back into the franchise now, rather than this dark, depressin, and dull kick that the franchise has been on. I've also always felt that Craig's take was lacking, although he's a brilliant actor. His take has the brooding dial turned up a little to high, and the charm, and humor dials turned down way to low.

~Heavy spoilers~üçø ~Long Read~üìñ  I think the overall problem with this movie is that they are trying to pick up the pieces left behind from spectre, and none of whats in that movie landed or felt legitimate or earned. I Actually like Lea Seydoux as an actress who played Madeline and was excited that she was the new bond girl in spectre, but the execution of their love story was so contrived, that in the end I really didn't care or believe it was a legitimate romance. Even the people who made that movie knows what's wrong with it. Why would they double down on it?   The writing and execution in the first quarter of the this movie was pretty good up to a point but the basis for the characters just weren't there for me, again cuz it so heavily relies on spectre. You could call this spectre 2 tbh   The concept of Bond having a child and the fact that it's a daughter on top of it Is actually a genius move as a concept on paper, its kinda like poetic justice for him. The idea that he has to really commit and care and actually be responsible for someone more than yourself. Having that character be his daughter could have been deeply profound in that way. They could have also addressed his relationship with women through the daughter character. That would be addressing the misogyny and faults in his character. Maybe even could address the vesper Lind history that made Bond unable to commit to any real relationships with women because of how his heart was basically ripped out of his chest by Vesper. I'll give Daniel Craig this, he knocked it out of the park with this movie in terms of his acting, especially in a few key scenes. He made me really feel like he realises his life has just turned upside down the moment he saw his daughters eyes. He genuinely made me tear up couple times in the film if I'm being honest. Any scene with Bond and his daughter was elevated by Craig's acting, and almost made up for some of the lackluster writing in the film. He made me really feel Bonds urgency and angst from her safety being in jeopardy. The daughter aspect, if it was utilised more and written better, could have had the potential to make this something really special and powerful. You could potentially really disect Bond through the daughter character, break his character down, address his issues and still feel like everything organically came together, cuz there could be a central focal point for it all, his new daughter. Come to think of it, it's actually not that dissimilar to the concept of what they did for wolverine in Logan.   Ok...Ana de armas was a total smoke show in this role! Holy hell she was good. Her character was badass, playful and intrigueing and that made her even sexier. I didn't think she could get more attractive but this role definitely did it. She should have been Bonds sidekick throughout the movie and become friends with him instead, moving away from a potentially cliched romance. Maybe there could have been a throughline in the film with him becoming sort of her mentor along the way since he's pretty much the most seasoned agent and she's insecure with how good she is. You can even still have the "saying no" scene later in the movie as their partnership grows and maybe some awkwardness leading to a back and forth conversation. Maybe he tells her about Vesper. Wnatever. Something like that would have felt organic, served as character development for Bond and some more three-dimensional depth to her character. Makes the "saying no" moment maybe actually mean something in the movie rather than a throwaway comedic jab at Bonds character.  LaShawna Lynch was fine. The hate for this movie for her being 007 and ppl just assuming that this is more of her movie than James bonds or she's taking power away from Bond and she's taking over, screw feminism, PC agenda pushing bitches, etc etc... I never subscribed to any of that nonsense. It's almost like people are trained and groomed these days to react like that without ever giving anything a chance....hey wait a minuteü§î. I like her as an actress, was looking forward to see her performance. Problem is I never understood her character's initial hostility with Bond, I was like why is she going undercover and manipulating him into bringing her back to his place just to stick it to him and show him up. Could of had the same conversation in a different way maybe in a conventional way, idk doesn't matter. I have a feeling that her character doesn't like Bond for his history of misogyny and filandering, but nothing about him as an agent. So the writers prolly decided they should have her characters main reason for not liking bond to actually be a general critique of men in society??? not cuz she has a grudge against him, or he always sucked at his job, or from some personal altercation with him. That's the type of shoehorning of PC agendas that sane ppl of the audience are talking about. Give her a real reason not to like Bond and you can still have her give him shit about his relationship with women. Also you'd think they would have just started out as platonic competition instead of trying to one up and literary sabotage each other's missions when they both have literally the same goal, especially when the stakes are potentially so high. You think maybe she would look up to bond a little, wouldn't the new agents and recruits look up to bond? Wouldn't they all want to secretly be like him, not as a person, but as an agent? Since when is going by the book and putting down your legendary predecessors an interesting character trait. Aren't you supposed to like her by the end of the movie? They didn't give any real scene or event that gets them to respect each other to follow through on squashing the animosity, so what was the whole point? At least Naomi Harris's character didn't have all this nonsense. No newcomer is just going to give a middle finger to the previous guy when he's freaking James Bond, he should be a legend amongst new agents. This whole thing of Bond being out of his time and too old was addressed in Skyfall and spectre so that stuff felt tired. I have no problem with the ideals that were being pushed to be PC and give Bond some me too'ing, but the execution is too forced. At least itsl not as bad as Disney's Star wars tho. There is a way to do this people, organically, where it doesn't feel like a soapbox.  Side note, felt the action was a bit underwhelming in this movie. It peaked with the scene in the first half with that goon shooting away at Madeline's window in the car and James just letting it happen while he deliberates wether to trust her or not. Great Bond moment and was narratively creative. This whole scene is totally Bond and badass. You believe Bond would be an asshole and do something like that. For the most part though this series has lost its edge especially in the action scenes, starting with some parts of Skyfall and heavily with spectre. They just came across as made to look pretty and expensive and putting more effort into the camera work trying to get everything in one one shots rather than making the action actually exciting. You guys remember the parkour scene leading to the shootout at the embassy in Casino Royale?! Those action scenes have so much edge to them, you feel the danger bond is in. Editing is one of the most important components to action scenes and these just feel like constantly unnecessary long takes. Now we even have parts  where he is just walking right out in the open, firing his gun vaguely in bad guys direction, on a set that probably costed 1/4 of the movies budget. Even holding his gun from the hip at times and still casually just hitting his mark every time. All the while with the bond theme blaring in the background. Did the same thing in spectre at the desert scene. Just seemed like a total waste and honestly a bit lazy. The SUV Chase was ok I guess but it has Bond literally nudging his car into the bad guys cars just once and they go flying in to some convenient obstacle or hit a ramp that launches them into a flip. A lot of the action is like this and it comes off as just lazy even though they spend hundreds of millions of dollars making these movies.  All that being said I didn't hate the movie, if you watch it just as a spy drama it's not that bad. I didn't get bored really that much even tho it's almost 3 hrs. So take that for what it's worth. There's annoying bits of comedy and one liners that come off as awkward rather than funny. Always thought that kind of stuff didnt fit for Craig's films. Rami Malek was good, but underrutilized. His characters ultimate goal was not really clearly defined, at least to me, but I think he served his purpose in the movie. Nothing more nothing less. For me I didn't mind bond dieing, and the film for the most part earned it, was done decently enough.  It's certainly better than quantum of solace and spectre. A serviceable end to Daniel Craig's era. Although, I have noticed things about these last 3 films and they make me feel that ever since The Dark Knight came out, the people behind these films have been trying to copy Chris Nolan's aesthetics, that should stop. But that's a whole nother conversation.  Thanks for reading.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs because she‚Äôs a woman‚Äù  Clearly Chris doesn‚Äôt get the history of James Bond, the genre it‚Äôs centered on - and who the character is (his archetype).  Yes things can change with time. But people also have a right to not like when Hollywood is undoing character / genres for empty & hollow reasons.  Bond is also a cultural icon to the English. To me Idris Elba is a good example. Anyone outraged at him being Bond are idiots and can pound sand. Bond is a British male and there isn‚Äôt a more perfect choice for the original archetype than Elba. Skin color should not matter, especially when it‚Äôs actually very representative of the diversity of modern England.  Bond is not a woman tho. And yes Bond‚Äôs gender is a major aspect to who the character is, and what the genre of the film is. And there is nothing wrong with that.  Not all films need to be for everyone. The same way Moonlight should never be changed- because its about the experiences of a specific race and their struggles with their identity inside of the culture.  The left is always telling people to respect cultures and to not change things that important to those cultures. Yet people are not allowed to feel that way about Bond.  I get for many people in the West they only see Bond as a casual movie property and don‚Äôt care to understand the property that Ian Fleming made. But it is an institution and it does matter culturally to a lot of people.  Hollywood doesn‚Äôt give a sh** about this and you are fooling yourselves if you think they do things for righteousness and the goodness of their heart.  Edit: yes Bond‚Äôs gender is actually important to the character and the genre of the films. Which is why I said the outrage behind Elba was legitimately ridiculous. Because Skin color IS NOT pivotal to the character and never was.  I also know the new film isn‚Äôt about a female James Bond. Just addressing Stuckman‚Äôs throwaway nod implying all criticism is because of people being discriminatory. I don‚Äôt think he fully gets it and there is legit criticism to how the property is being handled (or rather worry over what the people running it are saying).

The problem wasn't that a "woman" wrote this... it's that they were constantly talking about how James Bond and his "toxic masculinity" needs to be addressed and how this character & the movies need to "stick with the times" and have more "diversity and inclusion" and whatnot. Yeah sorry, but rhetoric like that is a MAJOR red flag and the mere fact that this is what these writers prioritized rightly left a bad taste in people's mouth. I don't want to watch a Bond movie to be reminded for the millionth time that men are toxic and that masculinity is evil and that somehow, Bond's "playboy" behavior is part of the patriarchy. I honestly can't believe you think these messages are "genuine". Granted, I haven't seen the movie, so I'm hoping that the immense backlash was enough for them to get rid of these insane messages and simply make a GOOD MOVIE. But you make it seem like people were just flipping out for no reason. I'm sure if a director talked about something as insane as "white genocide" and was making a movie about race relations, you wouldn't be saying "well, just give them a chance". Once you delve into these radical (in this case, anti-male) ideologies, people have every right to be concerned. The fact that the movie ended up being fine or "genuine", is not an argument against the valid backlash beforehand. Especially when that backlash might have been the reason why they changed certain elements of the story.   Concerning the Lashana Lynch "controversy"... at the very beginning it was literally reported that they were seeking to replace the JAMES BOND character with a black female, for purely diversity's sake. THAT is what people objected to. Later on they changed it to her taking over the "007" role, which I personally don't have a problem with as it makes narrative sense for a new person to take that mantle... but let's not pretend like her being a black woman wasn't a highly deliberate creative choice to score social brownie points, b/c it obviously was. And if her character doesn't end up constantly downplaying Bond in the film, then I'm happy... b/c some of the early trailers really made it out to seem like she was just there to emasculate Bond and look down on him. A typical trope that Hollywood has employed for decades to arbitrarily represent female characters as "strong & independent", when they just come across as b*tchy and annoying.  Honestly though... I didn't even think of this whole "woke Bond" controversy b/c all that uproar literally happened almost two years ago. So I wouldn't even have thought about it going into this movie. The fact that you felt the need to properly address this, ironically puts me off more than anything... as I'm basically just hearing "well yeah, it's all in there, but they did it in a good way". Sorry, but I don't believe you can force the idea that masculinity is toxic down my throat "in a genuine way". Stuff like that has absolutely NO place in a Bond movie, especially not when it has always catered to a predominately male audience. Women are literally NEVER being lectured or insulted as a gender in movies. Anyone who disagrees, just name me ONE example that isn't a 100 year old movie. So why do these execs expect men to put up with this nonsense? I'm not saying I want women to be insulted. I'm saying I'm tired of going to see movies to be reminded of asinine politics beliefs that I absolutely do not subscribe to.

Definitely not my favourite of Craig's Bond films (Casino Royale and Skyfall are still way better), but I thought this was a very strong film, and a great conclusion to this era of the series. The action was spectacular and intense, the script had a great witty sense of humour (courtesy of Phoebe Waller-Bridge no doubt), the music was gorgeous, and the performances were all excellent. It has its share of issues, the villain is rather weak and suffers from confusing motivations, and the extended runtime does mean that the pacing does drag in a few places (especially during the middle). That said, it's still a fun ride on the whole, and a very fitting finale for Daniel Craig, who has definitely done a fantastic job in the role over the last 15 years. To quote M in the film (albeit much less tersely than he says it): "you've done your bit, and we thank you for your service."
